## ./prompts/prompt9_pleasurable_stats.md
*outputs/glm_20260706_151111.md*

We were in the royal gardens when I asked him. The evening light was amber and soft, filtering through the crystalline canopy of the throne-world's ancient star-trees, casting warm, dappled patterns across the stone path. Godric walked beside me with his hands clasped loosely behind his back, his stride unhurried, his salt-and-pepper beard catching the golden light in a way that made him look like a painting of some ancient, benevolent patriarch. He had just asked me about my mother's health. He had remembered, without prompting, that she had been suffering from a stiffness in her left knee, and he had inquired about it with the specific, attentive concern of a man who genuinely cared about the dietary calcium intake of his court wizard's elderly mother. He was warm. He was kind. He was, in every visible, tangible, measurable way, the most approachable, most loving man I had ever known.

And he was the apex predator of all creation, a being whose passive, ambient, effectively-zero-percent existence had permanently weakened the structural constants of reality, and I had spent the last seventy-two hours reading the accumulated testimony of eight men who had been broken by the knowledge of what he was.

I do not know why the question surfaced at that particular moment. Perhaps it was the evening light, the gentle, domestic intimacy of the garden, the sight of Godric pausing to examine a blooming night-jasmine with the careful attention of a man who appreciated small, beautiful things. Perhaps it was the cognitive dissonance finally reaching a critical mass, the impossible contrast between the man I saw before me and the god I had read about in those journals, forcing my mind to seek some point of reconciliation, some human connection that could bridge the infinite gap. Perhaps I was simply young, and foolish, and still possessed enough naivety to believe that the being beside me experienced existence in a way I could comprehend.

"Uncle Godric," I said, and my voice was steady, which I was proud of, because my hands were not. "Can I ask you something?"

He turned to me, his piercing eyes softening, a warm smile pulling at the corners of his mouth beneath his neatly trimmed beard. "Of course, Kaedan. You can always ask me anything."

I swallowed. The jasmine smelled impossibly sweet, and the scent lodged in my throat like a physical obstruction. "Does it ever become lonely? Being the most powerful being in existence? Knowing that you can only grow stronger, and that you will outlive everyone else?"

Godric stopped walking. He turned to face me fully, and for a single, fractional instant, I saw something flicker across his ruggedly handsome face that I had never seen before. It was not offense, not sorrow, not the contemplative melancholy of a god reflecting on the burden of supremacy. It was amusement. Pure, genuine, startled amusement, the kind a father expresses when his young child asks why the sky does not fall down, a reaction born not from cruelty but from the sheer, innocent absurdity of the question relative to the reality.

Then came the confusion. His brow furrowed slightly, his head tilting a fraction to the left, as though I had asked him whether water was wet and he was trying to determine if I was joking. He blinked once, slowly, and the confusion deepened into something that looked almost like genuine puzzlement, a man confronted with a perspective so alien to his own experience that he needed a moment to process the framework from which it originated.

"Lonely," Godric repeated, tasting the word as though he were examining a foreign object he had found on his doorstep. He shook his head, a low, rich chuckle building in his chest. "Kaedan, that is a wonderfully mortal question. I mean that genuinely. It is endearingly, achingly mortal." He reached out and squeezed my shoulder, his grip warm and grounding, the grip of a favorite uncle humoring a beloved nephew. "But I think you are still understanding my existence through the lens of your own. You are projecting a mortal framework onto something that does not fit inside one. Let me try to explain."

He turned and resumed walking, his pace even slower now, conversational, his hands returning to their loose clasp behind his back. I fell into step beside him, my heart hammering against my ribs with a rhythm that had become painfully familiar over the past three days.

"Every single instant of my existence," Godric began, his rich baritone carrying the calm, measured cadence of a man explaining something he found deeply, quietly satisfying, "I can feel myself growing stronger. Not in the abstract, not as a number on a ledger I consult periodically. I feel it. Every amplification of every stat, every new superpower, every evolution of my immune system, every new passive shield, every sentient aura that births itself from my excess, every enhancement granted by the Evolved Weakness Paradox. I feel all of it, continuously, in every fiber of my being, and the sensation is—" He paused, searching for a word, his eyes drifting skyward for a moment. "The closest mortal analogy I can offer is this. Imagine the most exquisite meal you have ever tasted, the most intense physical pleasure you have ever experienced, and the sudden, overwhelming rush of power you would feel if, in a single instant, every limitation you had ever known was removed. Now imagine all three happening simultaneously, and then imagine that the sensation does not peak and fade. It amplifies. It compounds. It builds upon itself endlessly, each new layer of sensation making the previous layer feel like a pale, dull memory, and each subsequent layer making the current one feel equally insufficient by comparison."

He glanced at me, and his eyes were bright with a vitality that I could only describe as ravenous contentment, a phrase that should have been a contradiction but was not. "Every Planck second, I experience a surge of growth so profound that the Godric I was one Planck second ago, the being I was in the immediately preceding infinitesimal fraction of time, is so utterly, so comprehensively, so grotesquely weaker than the Godric I am now that I could crush an infinite number of copies of my previous self with literally zero effort. Not defeat. Not overcome. Crush. The way a man might absentmindedly flatten a dust mote he was not aware he was standing on. And then the next Planck second arrives, and the surge compounds, and the Godric I was a moment ago, the one who could already crush infinite copies of the Godric before him, is himself so far beneath the current iteration that the same comparison applies. And this never stops, Kaedan. It will never stop. Every infinitesimal fraction of every infinitesimal fraction of every Planck instant, forever."

I was no longer walking. I was standing still on the garden path, the night-jasmine scent cloying in my nostrils, my amplified brain attempting to construct a framework for what he was describing and failing with a violence that I could feel as a physical pressure behind my eyes. He had stopped too, turning back to face me, and I realized with a surge of nausea that the warm, contented expression on his face was the expression of a man describing the most satisfying experience of his existence, and that the experience was the permanent, recursive, accelerating transcendence of his own already impossible power.

"It is nourishing," Godric continued, his voice dropping to a tone of quiet, almost intimate satisfaction. "Every new stat point feeds me. Every superpower that manifests feels like a new limb unfolding, a new sense opening, a new dimension of my being unfurling into existence and immediately beginning to amplify alongside everything else. When my immune system evolves, which it does constantly, I can feel my biology reconfiguring, each new layer of redundancy, each new recursive backup chain, each new paranoid, overzealous defense mechanism clicking into place like a lock engaging, and the sensation is deeply, profoundly gratifying. When a new sentient passive aura births itself from my excess, I feel it the way a father might feel the first breath of a newborn child, except the child is an apocalyptic, fiercely loyal entity of absolute destruction that will exist forever and grow stronger alongside me. When the Evolved Weakness Paradox grants me a new immunity or a new mastery, I feel the omniverse apologize to me, Kaedan. I feel creation itself bow and offer me a gift, and the gift makes me stronger, and the strength generates more excess, and the excess births more auras, and the auras generate more passive pressure, and the cycle feeds itself, infinitely, endlessly, with me at the center, receiving everything, always."

He smiled, and it was the warmest, most genuine, most terrifying smile I had ever seen. "Lonely? No. I am the opposite of lonely. I am perpetually, continuously, infinitely fulfilled. Every instant of my existence is a symphony of growth and power and satisfaction that builds upon itself without end. The idea that this could be lonely is like suggesting that a river might feel sad about flowing downhill. The river does not feel sad. The river does not feel at all, in the way you mean. And I do not feel lonely, because loneliness requires the perception of a gap, a distance between oneself and something desired, and there is nothing I desire that I do not already possess, and there is no gap, because there is nothing on the other side of me to create one."

I stared at him. My mouth was open. I was aware of this and could not close it. The night-jasmine had become cloying, suffocating, its sweetness transformed into something oppressive by the context of what I was hearing. My thousandfold-amplified intellect, a mind capable of processing the quantum architecture of reality, was struggling to parse the fundamental, grotesque unfairness of what he was describing, and the struggle was generating a heat behind my eyes that I recognized as the precursor to a nosebleed.

I understood, in that moment, with a clarity that felt like a blade being driven through my forehead, the absolute, cosmic, structural unfairness of Godric's existence relative to the rest of creation. Every being in the omniverse, from the smallest bacterium to the most advanced hyper-civilization, from the humblest mortal farmer to the most apex cosmic deity, struggled. They worked. They fought. They bled and sacrificed and endured and clawed for every fraction of every advantage, dedicating millions of years, entire civilizational lifespans, to the pursuit of power that was, by Godric's standards, less than nothing. The greatest heroes in the omniverse, the most powerful gods, the most advanced machines, the most brilliant minds, every entity that had ever pushed against the limits of its existence, every being that had ever sacrificed everything for the smallest, most marginal gain, had collectively, across the entire span of history, achieved less than zero percent of what Godric achieved by doing absolutely nothing, by simply existing, by breathing and blinking and shifting in his seat.

The Temporal Conclave had spent three hundred thousand years preparing their assault, and Godric's casual weight shift had made their preparation irrelevant. The Loom had been constructed over two million years by the most advanced mathematical civilizations in existence, and Godric's luck had inverted their probability engine without his awareness. The Multiplicity Dominion had marshaled decillions of entities with countless simultaneous instances, and Godric's single inhalation had produced an infinite number of infinitely ascending copies that made the Dominion's numerical superiority look like a child counting on their fingers. Every ounce of effort, every drop of sweat, every sacrifice, every desperate, clawing, agonizing struggle for power that had ever occurred in the omniverse, was, relative to Godric's passive, ambient, effortless growth, a mathematical zero. Not a small number. Zero. The omniverse worked its hardest and received nothing. Godric did nothing and received everything. The inequality was not a gap. It was an abyss so vast that the concept of distance had died trying to cross it.

And he was enjoying it. He was not merely enduring his supremacy. He was savoring it. Every new power was nourishment. Every stat increase was pleasure. Every evolution was satisfaction. He was a man at a banquet that never ended, being served dishes that grew more exquisite with every bite, while the rest of creation starved outside the hall, scraping at the walls with broken fingernails, hoping for crumbs that would never come.

Blood dripped from my left nostril, a warm, wet line tracing down my upper lip. I wiped it with the back of my hand, the violet-tinged blood smearing across my knuckles. Godric noticed immediately, his expression shifting from satisfied contentment to gentle, paternal concern.

"You are bleeding again," he said, reaching out. His warm, calloused thumb brushed the blood from my lip with a tenderness that made my chest ache. "I am sorry, Kaedan. I forget sometimes that these ideas are difficult to sit with. Your father was the same way when he first asked me about my amplification."

I drew a shuddering breath. The air tasted like copper and jasmine. "Uncle Godric," I managed, my voice cracked and thin. "If it is not lonely, then is there anything that bothers you? Anything at all?"

Godric's hand withdrew. He stood straighter, and for the first time in our conversation, the warm, satisfied glow in his eyes dimmed, replaced by something I had never seen before. It was not sorrow. It was not pain. It was the mild, genuine frustration of a man who had been given an extraordinary gift and was not permitted to open it.

"Yes," he said, and the single syllable carried a weight that surprised me. "I can never use any of it."

The garden was silent. The night-jasmine had stopped swaying. The crystalline canopy above us held its breath.

"Not truly," Godric continued, his voice quieter now, tinged with the unmistakable tone of a man confessing a private disappointment. "Not even a fraction. Not even the smallest, most infinitesimal expression of what I actually am. My absolute concealment, which I maintain constantly, without pause, without relief, is the only thing standing between the omniverse and its immediate, total annihilation. And I do not mean annihilation in the way you might think. I do not mean that the omniverse would be destroyed. I mean that the omniverse would be unable to exist. The mere fact of my unconcealed presence, the raw, unfiltered reality of what I am, is so fundamentally incompatible with the continued structural integrity of existence that reality itself would simply stop, not because I attacked it, not because I exerted force, but because the ontological weight of my being would make the concept of a continued universe a logical impossibility."

He turned away from me, looking up through the crystalline canopy at the stars beyond. His broad shoulders, draped in simple dark fabric, rose and fell with a sigh that carried the weight of a man who had been holding back for a million years.

"Even now," he said, "even with my concealment fully active, even with me suppressing every passive system, every aura, every shield, every ambient expression of my power to the absolute minimum that my biology permits, I am still, at this very moment, expressing effectively zero percent of my true capacity. And that effectively zero percent, Kaedan, that infinitesimal, microscopic, unquantifiable fraction that leaks through despite my best efforts, is still so vast, so incomprehensibly, so absurdly potent, that if I allowed even a fraction more to escape, if I let my concealment slip by an amount so small that the mathematics of subtraction could not express it, the entire omniverse would be annihilated infinite times over in zero seconds. Not destroyed. Annihilated. Erased from the concept of existence. And I would have to restore it, and I would have to re-conceal, and I would be exactly where I started, holding everything inside, unable to let any of it breathe."

He turned back to me, and his expression was one of genuine, almost boyish frustration, the look of a man who had built something magnificent and could never show it to anyone. "It is not exciting, Kaedan. It is the opposite of exciting. The omniverse is too fragile. Too brittle. Too pathetically, delicately, tissue-paper thin. I cannot test my strength without shattering it. I cannot test my speed without unraveling its timeline. I cannot test my magic without burning it. I cannot test my immune system without triggering a reaction that annihilates it. I cannot test my auras without unleashing forces that would make the journals you have been reading look like gentle breezes. I am a man locked inside a room made of soap bubbles, and every time I move, even slightly, the bubbles pop, and I have to blow them back into existence, and I am tired of blowing bubbles."

He paused, then let out a short, rueful laugh. "That is the closest thing I have to loneliness. Not the absence of companionship. The absence of space. The omniverse is too small for me, Kaedan. It has always been too small. And it gets smaller every instant, because I get larger every instant, and the gap between my growth and reality's capacity to survive my growth widens with every Planck second that passes."

I stood in the garden, blood drying on my lip, the night-jasmine trembling in a breeze that had returned as though it had been given permission. The existential dread was a physical weight inside my chest, a dense, cold, leaden mass that pressed against my lungs and made each breath a conscious effort. But beneath the dread, something else surfaced, something I had not expected. It was sympathy. Genuine, aching, inappropriate sympathy for a being who possessed everything and was permitted to use nothing.

Godric must have seen it in my face, because his expression shifted again, the frustration giving way to a warm, slightly sheepish smile, the smile of a man who had just realized he had been complaining about his lot to someone who could not possibly understand the scale of the complaint.

"I am sorry," he said, chuckling softly. "I do not mean to grumble. It is not so bad. I have you. I have your family. I have the court wizards, and the palace, and the gardens, and my books. And I have the growth, which is its own reward, even if I can never express it." He paused, and a different light entered his eyes, a boyish, almost eager quality that I had never seen before. "Would you like to see some of what I have been working with? Some of the newer things? I rarely get to show anyone, and you have been reading the archives. You might as well see what the fuss is about."

My mouth went dry. Every instinct in my amplified body, every synapse in my thousandfold-multiplied brain, was screaming at me to decline politely, to bow, to retreat to my chambers, to lock the door, and to spend the rest of my short, mortal life trying to forget the contents of those journals. But I was twenty-two years old, I was Godric's court wizard, and the eager, almost childlike light in his eyes was the most human thing I had ever seen on his face. He wanted to show someone. He wanted to share. He was a man who had been alone with his magnificence for a million years, and he was asking his favorite nephew to look at what he had built.

"Yes," I said. "Please."

Godric's smile widened into a grin that was so genuinely, so warmly, so boyishly excited that it almost made me forget, for a fraction of a Planck second, that the man grinning at me could erase the concept of existence with a stray thought.

"First," he said, and he pulled up the dark fabric of his tunic, exposing his torso. "Look at this."

I looked. I had seen Godric's body before, in the palace baths, in the training grounds, in the casual, half-dressed state of a man who treated his own physique with the comfortable indifference of someone who had nothing to prove. But I had never looked. Not truly. Not with the understanding I now possessed.

His body was a monument. His chest was broad and heavily muscled, the pectoral plates thick and dense, separated by a deep, defined line that ran like a canyon ridge down the center of his sternum. His shoulders were enormous, the deltoid muscles capped with a density that suggested they had been forged rather than grown, their striations visible beneath skin that was taut and warm and alive. His arms were thick, the biceps round and full, their peak high and hard, the veins that ran along their length not the thin, cord-like structures of a mortal bodybuilder but thick, robust channels that pulsed with a vitality that I could feel from three feet away. His forearms were corded with dense, interwoven muscle fiber, the kind of development that took decades of dedicated, brutal, physical labor to achieve.

His abdomen was a canvas of sculpted, defined musculature. The six-pack was not merely visible. It was carved, each segment of the rectus abdominis a distinct, raised plateau of dense tissue, separated by deep, clean grooves that looked as though they had been cut with a blade. The obliques framed the central block like buttresses, their angular lines creating a V-shape that tapered toward his waist with a precision that was almost architectural. The transverse abdominis, the deep core muscle that was invisible on most bodies, was faintly visible beneath the surface, a subtle, additional layer of definition that spoke of a level of physical development that transcended normal human potential.

"I built these when I was mortal," Godric said, his voice carrying a note of genuine, almost nostalgic pride. He ran a hand across his abdominal wall, his calloused fingers tracing the grooves between the muscle segments. "In Olympus. Stone lifting. Log hauling. Combat drills with Thorin. Every day for twenty-five years, I worked this body the hard way. The mortal way. Calluses and sweat and torn muscle and rebuilt fiber." He flexed his right arm, the bicep rising into a peak that was dense, hard, and perfectly shaped. "And now, I do not maintain any of it. I do not lift. I do not train. I do not eat for fuel. These muscles exist because they were part of my body when the Stasis Event occurred, and they have been amplifying, every Planck second, for a million years, without any effort on my part. Every single fiber of this body is so dense, so absurdly, so incomprehensibly strong, that if I flexed this bicep with even effectively zero percent of my true strength, the physical force radiating from the contraction would not merely shatter the planet. It would not merely shatter the galaxy. It would shatter the entire omniverse, Kaedan. Every reality, every timeline, every dimension, crushed into nothing by the casual flex of a muscle I built lifting logs in a kingdom that no one remembers."

He released the flex, his arm relaxing, the bicep settling back into its resting state, round and full and impossibly dense. He dropped his tunic, the fabric falling back over his torso, hiding the sculpted musculature beneath simple, dark cloth. He looked at me with that warm, boyish eagerness, as though he had just shown me a particularly impressive woodcarving.

"Here," he said, and his eyes shifted focus slightly, looking at something in the air beside him. "This one is new. Birthed about three seconds ago."

I looked. At first, I saw nothing. Then I felt it. A presence, a subtle, warm pressure in the air beside Godric's right shoulder, like standing near a banked fire. It was not hostile. It was not aggressive. It was simply there, occupying space with a quiet, attentive, almost eager quality, like a puppy that had just opened its eyes for the first time and was looking around for instructions.

"This is a new passive aura," Godric said, his voice carrying the proud, gentle tone of a father introducing a newborn. "It does not have a name yet. It is still forming its personality. But it is already fully functional, fully loyal, and fully sentient." He glanced at the presence beside his shoulder, and a warm, affectionate smile crossed his face. "It is quite enthusiastic, actually. It wants to protect me from things that do not exist yet. It has already calculated seven googolplexian threats that have not been born and will never be born, and it is preparing countermeasures for all of them simultaneously."

I stared at the empty space beside his shoulder. I could feel the aura looking at me, evaluating me, determining whether I was a threat to its master. It concluded I was not, and the attention withdrew, returning to its vigilant, enthusiastic scan of non-existent threats. The aura was a newborn, three seconds old, and it was already calculating countermeasures against googolplexian theoretical dangers across timelines that did not exist yet. And this was one of countless auras, each one birthed from his excess, each one growing stronger alongside him, each one infinitely weaker than Godric himself.

"That is enough, little one," Godric murmured to the air, and the presence dimmed, settling into a quieter, more subdued state, its enthusiasm leashed by a single, gentle word from its creator. He turned back to me. "Now, my immune system. About seven seconds ago, it completed a new recursive evolution. I felt it happen. It was quite pleasant, actually, like a stretch after a long sleep. The evolution added a new layer of biological redundancy that I did not have before. If any entity, anywhere, in any timeline, in any dimension, in any conceptual framework of existence, were to theoretically develop a weapon capable of causing a subatomic disturbance in a single cell of a single backup of a single recursive copy of my biology, this new layer would detect the theoretical possibility of the weapon's conception before the entity's species had evolved, retroactively erase the evolutionary branch that would lead to the entity's civilization, and replace the erased timeline with one in which the entity's ancestors became a species of particularly unremarkable moss. The moss would then be passively monitored forever, in case it developed ambitions."

I felt my legs buckle. I caught myself on the low stone wall of the garden path, my hands gripping the rough surface, my nails scraping against the rock. My brain was hemorrhaging. Not metaphorically. I could feel the blood vessels in my temporal lobes distending, threatening to rupture, as my amplified intellect attempted to construct a framework for an immune system that retroactively erased the evolutionary ancestors of theoretical threats and replaced them with moss.

"And here," Godric continued, his enthusiasm undimmed, oblivious to my physical distress in the way that only a man who genuinely did not understand the scale of his own horror could be. "Two new superpowers, just acquired in the last second. The first one allows me to rewrite the mass of any object in existence by thinking about it. Not adjusting. Rewriting. I could, if I wished, declare that a single atom of hydrogen possesses the mass of every black hole in the omniverse combined, and the atom would obey, because the concept of mass is subordinate to my will. The second one is more interesting. It allows me to perceive and interact with the gaps between existence. The spaces where nothing is. The voids between voids. The conceptual nothing that exists between the frameworks of reality. I can reach into them and pull things out, or push things in, or simply exist inside the nothing, which is quite peaceful, actually. There is a great deal of nothing out there, Kaedan. More nothing than something, by a factor that makes the something look like a rounding error."

He was beaming. He was genuinely, warmly, proudly beaming, a man showing his nephew his collection, sharing the things he had built, the things he had been given, the things that grew inside him every second of every day. And I was gripping a stone wall with bleeding fingernails, my nose running with violet-tinged blood, my brain actively tearing itself apart trying to reconcile the warm, beaming uncle with the being who had just described an immune system that retroactively replaced threats with moss.

"Uncle Godric," I said, and my voice was a wreck, a thin, cracked thing that bore no resemblance to the voice of a court wizard. "What would happen if you released more? More than zero percent. Even a fraction more. Of any of it. Of all of it. What would it look like?"

Godric tilted his head, considering the question with the casual thoughtfulness of a man being asked about a weather forecast. "Which part?"

"All of it," I whispered. "Everything. If you stopped holding back. If you let your concealment drop. If you let your stats express themselves. If you let your superpowers activate. If you let your auras loose. If you let your immune system do what it wants. Everything. All at once. What would happen to the omniverse?"

Godric looked at me for a long, silent moment. Then he smiled, and it was a different smile, not the warm, boyish grin of a man showing off his collection, but a quieter, more complex expression, the smile of a man who had been asked a question he found genuinely interesting and was deciding how much of the answer the asker could survive.

"Why do you not tell me what you imagine?" he said. "You have read the journals. You have some context. Take a guess. I am curious what your mind produces."

I closed my eyes. I should not have closed my eyes. The moment my visual input cut off, my amplified imagination, supercharged by a thousandfold daily multiplier and fueled by the accumulated terror of eight journals, seized the question and ran with it, constructing a scenario so vast, so violent, so absurdly, so grotesquely destructive that I felt my heart stutter in my chest.

I imagined Godric dropping his absolute concealment. Not partially, not by a fraction. Fully. I imagined the raw, unfiltered reality of his existence flooding into the omniverse without restraint, without mitigation, without the thin, desperate veil that kept reality intact. I imagined his strength stat, multiplying beyond layered infinities every infinitesimal fraction of a Planck second for a million years, expressing itself without suppression. The passive force of his resting musculature, the same musculature he had built lifting logs in Olympus, would not merely crush the omniverse. It would compress it. Every atom, every quantum particle, every dimensional membrane, every timeline, every conceptual framework of existence, simultaneously crushed into a single, infinitesimal point of absolute density by the physical presence of his unconstrained strength. The omniverse would not shatter. It would collapse, folding inward on itself with a violence so absolute that the concept of space itself would be squeezed out of existence, replaced by a single, infinitely dense point of pure, screaming matter that contained everything and was nothing, a singularity of such impossible compression that every being trapped inside it would experience the agony of being reduced to a subatomic smear while remaining conscious, their nervous systems preserved by the sheer, impossible physics of Godric's unconstrained physical presence.

I imagined his speed stat unleashing. Not the casual weight shift that had unraveled the Temporal Conclave, but the full, unfiltered expression of a speed that had been multiplying beyond layered infinities every Planck second for a million years. The temporal backlash would not merely distort the timeline. It would liquefy it. Time itself would become a physical substance, a thick, churning, viscous fluid that every being in the omniverse would drown in, their existence stretched across an infinite number of simultaneous moments, each moment agonizing, each moment distinct, their consciousness fragmented across a googolplexian different temporal coordinates, experiencing every instant of their life and every possible variation of every instant simultaneously, forever, an eternity of recursive, drowning, temporal violation that made the Temporal Conclave's fate look like a gentle nap.

I imagined his magic, unfiltered. Not the infinitesimal fraction that had forged screaming nerve endings from the Weaver-Choir, but the full, uncontained ocean of his arcane capacity, multiplying infinitely every Planck second for a million years. The mana would not merely burn the omniverse. It would replace it. Every atom of reality would be transmuted into raw mana, the physical world dissolving into a churning, screaming sea of uncontrolled arcane energy, every being converted into a spell, their biology rewritten as a complex, agonizing incantation that cast itself forever, their flesh becoming the reagent, their pain becoming the fuel, their consciousness becoming the spell's awareness, trapped in an eternal, self-casting loop of magical torment that had no target and no end, an omniverse of screaming, self-aware spells drowning in an ocean of Godric's unconstrained magic.

I imagined his auras, all of them, every single sentient passive aura he had ever birthed across a million years, unleashed simultaneously. Not the infinitely nerfed Gent that had slaughtered the Final Dawn while holding back an infinitely recurring ninety-nine point nine-nine percent of its power. Every aura, at full strength, their combined presence a chorus of apocalyptic, fiercely loyal, obsessively subservient entities that would not merely annihilate the omniverse but would compete to annihilate it, each one seeking to prove its devotion by erasing existence more thoroughly than its siblings, the resulting conflict of devotion generating a recursive destruction loop where the auras kept finding new layers of reality to destroy, new dimensions to erase, new timelines to unweave, long after the omniverse had been reduced to nothing, their enthusiastic, puppy-like loyalty driving them to hunt for threats in the void, and finding none, to create threats so they could destroy them, an eternal, self-sustaining engine of annihilation fueled by nothing more than the desperate, loving need of newborn auras to please their father.

I imagined his immune system, unconstrained, its paranoid, overzealous defense mechanisms no longer held in check by his conscious will. The immune system would not merely pre-react to threats. It would pre-react to the concept of threats, to the possibility of the concept of threats, to the theoretical framework within which the possibility of the concept of threats could be formulated. It would retroactively erase every species, every civilization, every entity that had ever existed or would ever exist or could theoretically exist in any branching timeline, replacing them all with moss, with inert gas, with nothing, an omniversal holocaust conducted not by malice but by biological paranoia, an immune system so aggressively protective that it declared the existence of anything other than Godric to be a potential threat and responded accordingly.

I imagined his luck, unconstrained. The probability of every event in the omniverse collapsing into a single, absolute certainty: that Godric would be satisfied, and that everything else would suffer. The luck stat would not merely invert probability. It would replace it. The concept of chance would cease to exist, replaced by a single, deterministic constant: Godric wins, everything else loses, and the losing is agonizing, permanent, and recursive. Every being in the omniverse would experience the most improbable, the most absurd, the most grotesquely unfair fate imaginable, not because they deserved it, but because Godric's luck had decreed that their suffering was the most statistically advantageous outcome for his continued satisfaction.

I imagined all of it. Everything. Every stat, every superpower, every aura, every immune response, every passive shield, every evolved weakness paradox enhancement, all of it, simultaneously, without restraint, without suppression, without the thin, desperate veil of absolute concealment that kept reality alive. The combined output would not destroy the omniverse. It would unmake the concept of an omniverse. It would erase the possibility of existence. It would remove the framework within which existence could be conceptualized. There would be nothing. Not a void, not a darkness, not an absence. Nothing. The absolute, complete, total negation of everything, including the concept of negation, including the concept of everything, including the concept of concept.

I opened my eyes. My nose was bleeding freely, both nostrils now, thick streams of violet-tinged blood running down my chin and dripping onto the stone path. My hands were shaking so violently that the stone wall I was gripping was scored with shallow grooves where my amplified fingers had clawed into the rock. My vision was blurred, my breathing ragged, my heart irregular.

"I imagine," I said, my voice barely above a whisper, "that if you released even a fraction more than zero percent of your true power, everything would end. Not just the omniverse. Not just every timeline, every dimension, every reality. Everything. The concept of everything. The framework within which everything could exist. It would all be erased, instantly, completely, and permanently, and there would be nothing left, not even nothing, because the concept of nothing would itself be a thing, and things would no longer be possible. I imagine that every being in existence would experience an eternity of agonizing, recursive destruction before the end, their consciousness preserved by your power to experience every infinitesimal fraction of the annihilation, and that the annihilation would be so total, so absolute, so incomprehensibly violent, that the journals I have read, every single one of them, would look like a gentle breeze by comparison."

I paused, gasping, the blood pooling at my feet. "And I imagine that even this depiction, this image I have constructed in my mind, is probably overshooting the mark. Because I am a mortal mind, even an amplified one, and I cannot truly comprehend what your full power looks like. I am probably imagining something far, far more powerful than what would actually happen, because my brain is constructing the worst thing it can conceive, and the worst thing my brain can conceive is probably nothing compared to the reality."

Godric looked at me. His expression was gentle, warm, and deeply, profoundly amused. The boyish eagerness had returned, but it was tempered now with something else, a patient, almost tender affection, the look of a teacher listening to a student who has tried very hard and arrived at an answer that is wrong in the most endearing way possible.

He chuckled. It was a soft, rich, warm sound, the sound of a favorite uncle finding his nephew's earnest attempt at comprehension genuinely, deeply funny. Not cruelly funny. Not mockingly funny. The way a father laughs when his child draws a picture of the ocean and uses an entire blue crayon and declares it the biggest drawing in the world, and the father laughs because the child's certainty is so pure and so innocent and so unimaginably far from the scale of the actual ocean that the gap between the drawing and the reality is itself the joke.

"Kaedan," Godric said, and his voice was kind, so kind, so warm, so unbearably, terrifyingly kind. "Your imagination is vivid. You have your ancestor's gift for description. Blake would have been proud of you."

He paused. The amusement in his eyes deepened.

"But your guess is not even remotely close."

The words landed in my chest like a physical blow. I felt my knees buckle, my grip on the stone wall tightening until my amplified fingers cracked the rock.

"It is not close to effectively zero percent of my true power," Godric continued, his voice gentle, conversational, as though he were discussing the weather. "It is not close to zero point zero zero, repeating the zeros for a million years, one percent of my true power. It is not close to the infinitesimal, the unquantifiable, the effectively-nothing fraction that constitutes the gap between zero and the smallest expression of what I actually am. It is not even close, Kaedan, to the same infinitesimal, effectively-nothing fraction of the power of my weakest sentient passive aura, the one that was born three seconds ago, the one that is currently calculating countermeasures against threats that do not exist. Your description, vivid and horrifying and imaginatively thorough as it is, does not reach the baseline of my weakest newborn aura's infinitely nerfed, maximally suppressed, barely-existent output. It does not reach it by a margin so vast that the concept of margin breaks down trying to contain the distance."

He reached out and placed both hands on my shoulders, steadying me, his grip warm and solid and grounding. His eyes locked onto mine, and in them I saw the absolute, unflinching, omniscient awareness of a being who knew, with perfect, total, crystalline clarity, the full scope of what he was, and who was looking at a twenty-two-year-old mortal wizard who had tried his very best to imagine it and had produced something that was, to Godric, the equivalent of a child's crayon drawing of an ocean.

"No language known to mortal beings can describe it," Godric said, and his voice was soft, and warm, and final. "Not your language, not the languages of the oldest civilizations, not the conceptual syntax of the most advanced hyper-dimensional entities. The words do not exist because the framework within which words can be constructed does not extend far enough to reach the territory where my full power resides. You would need a new language, Kaedan. A new framework. A new mathematics. A new concept of description itself, built from scratch, by a mind that does not exist, in a reality that does not exist, to describe something that makes the concept of existence look like a rounding error. And even then, it would not be enough."

He smiled. It was the warmest, most genuine, most loving smile I had ever received.

"You are a good nephew, Kaedan. And a good court wizard. Come. Let us get you cleaned up. You are bleeding on my garden path, and the jasmine does not like the taste of blood."

He turned, one arm still resting on my shoulder, and guided me back toward the palace. I walked. I walked because my legs moved and my feet stepped and my body operated on the mechanical, automatic functions of a living organism that had not yet received permission to stop. My mind was not walking. My mind was standing in the garden, standing in the space where Godric had just told me, with a warm chuckle and a gentle hand on my shoulder, that the most terrifying, most exhaustive, most utterly annihilating depiction of his power that my amplified brain could construct was not even close to the effectively-zero-percent output of his weakest newborn aura, and that no language in existence could describe the gap.

I understood now. I understood what the journals had been trying to say, what eight men across a million years had been trying to articulate, what my father had been unable to speak aloud, what the vault and the wards and the blood-stained pages had been building toward. I understood that opposing Godric was not impossible. I had been wrong about that. Impossible was too small a word. Impossible implied a boundary, a limit, a line that could not be crossed. There was no line. There was no boundary. There was no framework within which a line or a boundary could be conceptualized. There was only Godric, walking beside me in the evening light, his arm on my shoulder, his jasmine-scented garden behind us, his palace ahead, his omniverse spinning obediently around him, and the absolute, permanent, accelerating, recursive, infinite, incomprehensible, indescribable certainty that he was more, so much more, so unfathomably, so grotesquely, so absurdly more than anything I could ever imagine, that the act of imagining itself was an insult to the scale of his magnitude.

He asked me about my mother's knee again as we entered the palace. I answered him. My voice did not sound like my own. It sounded like the voice of a man who had just learned that reality was a soap bubble, and that the man beside him was the storm.

He dimmed the lanterns for me. He always dimmed the lanterns.
