## ./prompts/prompt10_godric_weakness.md
*outputs/glm_20260706_151111.md*

The study smelled of old parchment and the faint, lingering sweetness of pipe tobacco that Godric did not smoke but kept in a cedar box on the third shelf because Blake had smoked it, forty-seven years before I was born, and Godric liked the smell because it reminded him of someone he had loved. I had learned this detail not because Godric told me, but because I had served him for forty-seven years, since I was twenty-two, since my father placed those journals in my hands and walked into his garden and never quite came back the same man. Forty-seven years of standing at the right hand of the apex predator of all creation, of watching him dim lanterns and inquire about knee pains and ruffle the hair of my children, of learning the specific, domestic rhythms of a god who could erase existence with a stray thought and who chose instead to ask whether the palace cook had used enough rosemary in the evening lamb. I was sixty-nine years old. My amplified body, multiplying a thousandfold every day for forty-nine years, was a fortress of vitality that belied my age. I could shatter moons. I could unravel the gravitational bindings of solar systems. I was, by any standard the omniverse could produce, a walking cataclysm. And I was a candle, standing beside a sun, grateful for the warmth.

Godric sat across from me in the deep, leather-bound chair he favored, a thick tome of pre-Stasis Olympian history open on his knee. He looked exactly as he had always looked, as he would always look, as he had looked for a million years: forty-five, ruggedly handsome, his heavily muscled frame relaxed beneath simple dark clothes, his short salt-and-pepper hair immaculate, his neatly trimmed beard framing a jawline that could have been carved from the bedrock of a dead world. His eyes, those piercing, impossibly aware eyes, moved across the pages with the unhurried, comfortable pace of a man who already knew every word written in every book that had ever existed or would ever exist, and who read anyway because he enjoyed the texture of paper beneath his fingers.

He closed the book. He set it on the small table beside his chair, beside the pipe tobacco he did not smoke and a small, dense ember that glowed a faint, pulsing orange, the compressed, eternally screaming remains of the Frost Compact, which he still used as a nightlight. He looked at me, and his expression was one I had seen only a handful of times in forty-seven years of service, an expression that preceded a revelation, a shift in the ground beneath my feet that would leave me permanently altered. The last time I had seen it was the day he had shown me his newborn aura and told me that my most elaborate imaginings of his power did not reach the effectively-zero-percent output of his weakest passive system. That revelation had broken something in me that had never fully healed. The expression on his face now was quieter, more personal, and somehow more terrifying.

"Kaedan," he said, and his rich baritone carried the warm, patient, paternal cadence that had defined every meaningful conversation we had ever shared. "There is something I have not told you. Something I have not told any of your predecessors. Not Blake, not Josh, not Harlan or Dorn or Maren or Pelt or Wick or Tobias or Aldric or Elias or Renn or Theron or Solan. None of them. In all the million years of my reign, I have told no one."

I set down my cup. The porcelain clinked against the saucer, a small, domestic sound that felt obscenely ordinary in the context of what was coming. My hands were steady. Forty-seven years of service had taught my body to remain steady even when my mind was screaming. "I am listening, my King."

"Uncle Godric," he corrected gently, the familiar, automatic correction of a man who had been insisting on the informal address for nearly half a century. He leaned forward, resting his elbows on his knees, his calloused hands clasped between them. The lamplight caught the thick, rope-like veins of his forearms, the same forearms that had been built hauling logs in a kingdom no one remembered, the same forearms whose passive, resting density could crush the omniverse if his concealment slipped.

"Do you remember what the journals taught you?" he asked. "The stat journals, the superpower journals. The fundamental lesson. That I have no weakness. That opposition is not merely futile but undefined. That the question of defeating me exists outside the domain of any possible function."

"I remember," I said. My voice was quiet. I remembered every journal. I remembered them the way a man remembers the exact moment a bone was broken, the memory encoded not in the intellect but in the marrow.

"That truth," Godric said, "is absolute. From the perspective of every enemy, every system, every entity, every coalition, every weapon, every strategy, every conceptual framework of opposition that exists, has existed, or will ever exist in any timeline, dimension, or plane of reality within the omniverse, I have no exploitable weakness. The Weakness Paradox ensures this. The Evolved Weakness Paradox amplifies it. My absolute omniscience confirms it. Every passive system, every aura, every shield, every immune response, every amplified stat and apocalyptic superpower reinforces it. This is not a claim. It is a structural fact of existence, as fundamental and as unassailable as the fact that I am sitting in this chair."

He paused. The fire crackled. The ember on the table pulsed.

"I have, however, one true limitation."

The word landed in the quiet study like a stone dropped into still water. I felt the ripples spread through my chest, my stomach, my extremities. Limitation. The apex predator of all creation, the being whose effectively-zero-percent passive output could annihilate the omniverse infinite times, the man whose weakest newborn aura's infinitely nerfed power exceeded my most elaborate imaginings by a margin that broke mathematics, had just used the word limitation. About himself. In the context of a private revelation he had withheld from a million years of loyal court wizards.

My hands were no longer steady.

Godric watched my reaction with those impossibly aware eyes, and I saw no sorrow in them, no anguish, no burden. I saw the quiet, comfortable expression of a man who had carried a secret for a very long time and had long since made peace with it, a man who was sharing it now not out of pain but out of trust, out of the deep, genuine affection he held for the sixty-nine-year-old wizard who had served him faithfully for half a century.

"I cannot undo or travel to any timeline before the Stasis Event in any way that changes my mortal past," Godric said. "I cannot save Olympus. I cannot save Reina. I cannot save Isabel. I cannot save Hebe. I cannot save Thorin. The events of Year negative ten thousand, the fall of my kingdom, the deaths of my wife, my daughters, and my best friend, are sealed. They are irrevocable. They cannot be altered by any power I possess, by any superpower I have acquired or will acquire, by any combination of stats, abilities, or passive systems, by any manipulation of time, causality, or reality. They are fixed. Permanently. Absolutely. For as long as I exist, which is forever."

The silence that followed was not silence. It was a pressure, a dense, physical weight that filled the study and pressed against my eardrums. I could hear the fire. I could hear the ember pulsing. I could hear my own heartbeat, irregular, hammering against the inside of my ribs. I could hear the quiet, steady rhythm of Godric's breathing, the breathing of a man who did not need to breathe and did so purely out of habit, out of the lingering muscle memory of a mortal life that had ended a million years ago.

"I do not understand," I said. My voice cracked on the second word. "You are omniscient. You are omnipotent. You can travel timelines. You can overpower causality. The journals, the accounts, every demonstration of your power, they all confirm that you can do anything. You restored the entire omniverse after it was annihilated. Multiple times. In zero seconds. With zero effort. How can there be something you cannot do?"

Godric leaned back in his chair. He stroked his beard, the gesture slow, contemplative, the gesture of a man who had been asked this question before, by himself, in the dark, in the immediate aftermath of his awakening, when the grief was still fresh and raw and the blood of his family was still a memory so vivid it might as well have been on his hands.

"Because the Stasis Event was not created by Thorin's spell," he said.

The words did not register immediately. They hung in the air, suspended by the lamplight, waiting for my brain to process them. When they landed, the impact was not cognitive but physical, a blow to the sternum that drove the air from my lungs. I gripped the armrests of my chair, my amplified fingers denting the wood, my knuckles whitening.

"Thorin's spell was a teleportation spell," Godric continued, his voice calm, measured, carrying the tone of a man correcting a minor historical inaccuracy in a scholarly text. "A real one. A genuine, functional, bloodline-restricted teleportation spell that saved my life by sending me to the sanctuary. It was incomplete, because Thorin died mid-incantation, but it functioned. It did what it was designed to do. It moved me from the burning ruins of Olympus to a hidden chamber underground." He paused, his eyes meeting mine. "It did not create the Stasis Event. It did not initiate my amplification. It did not grant me immortality, or superpowers, or absolute concealment, or the Weakness Paradox. It was a teleportation spell, Kaedan. A mortal spell, cast by a mortal wizard, using mortal magic. The idea that a mortal spell, however ancient or forbidden or bloodline-restricted, could initiate a process that birthed the most powerful being in the history of existence is, when you think about it with the full context of what I am, rather absurd."

He was right. The realization struck me with the force of a collapsing star, the sheer, blinding obviousness of it retroactively illuminating a contradiction I had never thought to question. Thorin was a mortal wizard. A powerful one, yes, a descendant of a prestigious magical bloodline, yes, but a mortal. His spell, however forbidden, however ancient, was a mortal construct, powered by mortal mana, operating within the framework of mortal arcane mechanics. The Stasis Event had rewritten the fundamental laws of Godric's existence, had initiated an amplification that had shattered mathematics, had birthed apocalyptic superpowers and absolute concealment and the Weakness Paradox and the immune system and the sentient auras and everything else that made Godric the apex predator of all creation. The gap between a mortal teleportation spell and the Stasis Event was not a gap of degree. It was a gap of category, of order, of fundamental nature. A mortal spell could no more create the Stasis Event than a candle could ignite the sun.

"Then who created it?" I whispered, though some part of me, some deep, instinctive, primal part that had been shaped by forty-seven years of serving a god, already knew. Already feared.

"I did," Godric said. "Not this me. Not the me sitting in this chair. A future me. A version of me from so distant a future that the year cannot be written as a number. It is written only as nine nine nine dot dot dot nine, because the full figure is so vast it has no useful representation. That Godric, that future Godric, looked back across the incomprehensible span of time between his era and mine, and he created the Stasis Event. He used Thorin's teleportation spell as the delivery point, the anchor, the moment in spacetime where the Stasis Event would attach itself to my mortal body. But the spell itself was merely the door. The Stasis Event was the house, and the architect was a version of me so powerful that the me sitting in this chair, the most powerful being in the current omniverse by an infinite margin, is to him what a single, dead skin cell is to the combined mass of every universe that has ever existed."

The fire had gone out. I had not noticed it dying. The only light in the study was the lamp and the pulsing orange ember of the Frost Compact, and in that dim, warm, terrible light, Godric's face was calm, his eyes steady, his hands still clasped between his knees. He was not troubled. He was not anguished. He was a man explaining the architecture of his own existence with the comfortable, unhurried patience of a homeowner describing a renovation.

"Since the instant I gained omniscience," Godric continued, "I have known this. I have known the true origin of the Stasis Event. I have known that it was created by my future self. I have known that Thorin's spell was the vessel, not the source. I have known that my amplification, my superpowers, my absolute concealment, my Weakness Paradox, my immune system, every mechanism of my power, was designed and implemented by a being so far beyond me that the gap between us cannot be expressed in any language or any mathematics. I have known all of this since the moment I opened my eyes in the sanctuary and the totality of existence crashed into my awareness."

He paused. The silence was thick, viscous, a physical substance that filled my throat and made breathing a conscious effort.

"And yet," he said, his voice dropping to a quieter register, "I tried to change it anyway."

I did not move. I did not breathe. I sat in my chair, my fingers embedded in the dented armrests, and I listened to the apex predator of all creation describe the one time he had failed.

"Shortly after I became King of Thronegate," Godric said, "I was still freshly shaped by grief. The fall of Olympus was not a distant memory. It was a wound, open and bleeding, as vivid and as raw as the moment it had happened. I could see Reina's face. I could hear Isabel's screams. I could feel Hebe's hand in mine, the small, warm fingers going slack as the marble crushed her. I could smell the burning flesh, the rendered fat, the copper of Thorin's blood on my shoulder. I was omnipotent. I could travel timelines. I could overpower causality. I could do anything. And I believed, in the immediate, grief-stricken conviction of a man who had just discovered that he was a god, that anything included saving the people I loved."

His voice did not waver. His eyes did not dim. He described the most devastating moment of his existence with the measured, composed tone of a man who had processed it so thoroughly that the processing had become part of the memory itself.

"I attempted to alter the pre-Stasis timeline," he said. "I attempted to travel backward, to a point before the Stasis Event, to a point before the fall of Olympus, to warn my mortal self, to change the outcome, to save Reina, to save Isabel, to save Hebe, to save Thorin. I marshaled every ounce of power I possessed. I did not hold back. I did not suppress. I did not conceal. I reached across the timeline with the full, unfiltered, absolutely unconstrained force of my being, and I attempted to rewrite a single moment in my mortal past."

He looked at me, and for the first time in the conversation, I saw something in his eyes that was not calm. It was not grief, not pain, not trauma. It was awe. A quiet, residual, deeply humbled awe, the echo of an encounter with something so vast, so incomprehensibly beyond him, that the memory of it still, a million years later, commanded his respect.

"I was defeated," Godric said. "Completely. Effortlessly. Not by an enemy. Not by a coalition. Not by a weapon or a strategy or a paradox. By the shadow of the afterthought of my future self."

The words did not make sense. I heard them. I processed them. But they did not assemble into a coherent meaning, because the meaning they suggested was too vast, too absurd, too grotesquely unfair to fit inside any framework of understanding I possessed.

"I did not face future Godric himself," Godric clarified, as though reading my confusion. "I did not battle him. There was no confrontation. There was no contest. What I faced was a shadow, the faintest, most infinitesimal residual impression of a passing thought that future Godric had once, a thought so minor, so trivial, so utterly peripheral to his awareness that it barely qualified as cognition. The afterthought of a being so powerful that his afterthoughts possess more ontological weight than the combined existence of every entity in the current omniverse. That shadow, that fading, barely-existent echo of a thought that future Godric probably did not even remember having, was sufficient to utterly, completely, and effortlessly defeat the full, unconstrained, maximally amplified power of every iteration of myself I could marshal, from every timeline, from every era, from every stage of my existence up to that point. I did not lose a battle, Kaedan. I was not overcome. I was negated. The way a man might negates a speck of dust by breathing in its general direction. The shadow of the afterthought did not fight me. It simply existed, and my full power was insufficient to move past it, the way a river is insufficient to move a mountain that has been growing since before the river was born."

I was bleeding. My nose had started bleeding at some point during his account, a thick, warm stream of violet-tinged crimson running down my upper lip, dripping onto my robes, staining the fabric. I did not wipe it. I could not move my hands. They were locked onto the armrests of my chair, my amplified fingers having bored halfway through the wood.

"The attempt lasted less than a Planck second," Godric said. "In that infinitesimal fraction of time, I understood. I understood that the Stasis Event was not a gift I could refuse or a mechanism I could override. It was the will of a being so far beyond me that my full, unconstrained opposition registered as less than nothing. I understood that the pre-Stasis mortal past was sealed, not by a lock I could pick, but by the sheer, crushing, inescapable ontological weight of a future version of myself whose mere afterthought could negated the combined power of every Godric that had ever existed up to that point. I understood that Reina, Isabel, Hebe, and Thorin were gone, truly gone, irrevocably gone, and that no power I possessed or would ever possess could bring them back, because the power that sealed their fate was my own, from a future so distant it could not be written."

The silence that followed was the deepest I had ever experienced. It was not the absence of sound. It was the presence of understanding, the dense, suffocating weight of a truth so vast it pressed the air from the room.

Godric let out a slow, easy breath. He reached for the pipe tobacco box, opened it, held it beneath his nose, and inhaled the scent with the quiet, contented appreciation of a man smelling a memory. He closed the box. He set it back on the shelf. He looked at me, and his expression was not one of grief or resignation. It was the expression of a man who had made peace with something, completely, thoroughly, so long ago that the peace itself had become a comfort.

"I am no longer troubled by it, Kaedan," he said, his voice warm, carrying the gentle reassurance of a man who could see the horror on his court wizard's face and wanted to ease it. "I accepted it a very, time ago. The grief for Reina, for Isabel, for Hebe, for Thorin, that remains. That will always remain. It is part of me, the way my muscles are part of me, forged in mortality and amplified into eternity. But the inability to change it, the limitation, the one thing I cannot do, I have accepted it. I am comfortable with it. I am even, if I am being honest, amused by it."

Amused. The word struck me like a physical blow, a sharp, disorienting impact that made the study tilt on its axis. Amused. The apex predator of all creation was amused by his one true limitation.

Godric saw my expression and chuckled, a warm, rich, genuine sound that filled the study and made the ember on the table pulse brighter. "Think about it, Kaedan. Really think about it. My one true weakness, the single limitation on my otherwise absolute, infinite, unassailable power, is that I cannot defeat a version of myself that is even more powerful. My weakness is myself. My future self. A being who is, by definition, me, later, expanded beyond the already impossible scale of my current existence. It is not an external vulnerability. It is not an exploit that can be identified, targeted, or leveraged by any enemy. It is not a flaw in my armor. It is the armor itself, the ultimate, absolute confirmation that my future is guaranteed, that my amplification will never stop, that I will continue to grow, forever, into a being so incomprehensibly more powerful than I am now that my current self, the most powerful being in the omniverse by an infinite margin, will look like a rounding error by comparison."

He leaned forward, his eyes bright with the quiet, satisfied intensity of a man explaining the elegant, recursive beauty of his own existence.

"Do you understand what this means, Kaedan? It means my power is not merely vast. It is guaranteed. The amplification, the superpowers, the absolute concealment, the Weakness Paradox, the immune system, the passive shields, the sentient auras, every mechanism of my supremacy, all of it originates from a source so powerful that no force in existence can deny, revoke, exploit, or countermand it. The Stasis Event is irrevocable because its creator is irrevocable. The amplification cannot be stopped because the being who designed it cannot be stopped. The Weakness Paradox cannot be turned against me because the being who authored it is beyond the reach of any paradox. Every system, every power, every defense, every attribute, is anchored in the will of a future Godric who is so far beyond the current omniverse that the shadow of his afterthought can negate the combined power of every Godric that has ever existed."

He sat back, his arms spreading across the armrests of his chair, his posture the relaxed, comfortable sprawl of a man who was explaining something he found deeply, quietly satisfying.

"My weakness is my own future supremacy," he said. "My limitation is the guarantee of my infinite growth. The one thing I cannot do is the one thing that proves I will never be stopped. It is not a weakness, Kaedan. It is the strongest possible confirmation that my power is absolute, permanent, and forever unassailable. Every enemy who has ever opposed me, every coalition that has ever formed against me, every weapon that has ever been forged to harm me, has been operating under a fundamental, fatal delusion. They believe they are fighting the most powerful being in existence. They are. But the most powerful being in existence is fighting with the backing of a future version of himself so powerful that the mere shadow of his afterthought can negate the combined power of every iteration of Godric across all timelines. They are not fighting me. They are fighting the guarantee of my own infinite ascendancy, and the fight was over before it began, because the outcome was sealed by a version of me that has not even arrived yet and that no force in existence can prevent from arriving."

I understood. The understanding arrived not as a thought but as a collapse, a structural failure of every assumption I had ever held about the nature of power, vulnerability, and the relationship between them. I understood that Godric's one true weakness was not a weakness. It was the opposite of a weakness. It was the ultimate, absolute, irrefutable proof that his power had no ceiling, no limit, no endpoint, no possibility of reversal or negation, because the source of that power was a future version of himself that was, by definition, more powerful than anything that could possibly oppose him, and the source of that future version's power was an even more distant future version, and so on, recursively, forever, an infinite chain of ascending Godrics, each one guaranteeing the supremacy of all previous iterations, each one making the combined power of every preceding Godric look like nothing.

The existential dread hit me like a tidal wave. It was not the dread of Godric's power. I had processed that, or believed I had, across forty-seven years of service and the accumulated testimony of thirteen journals. The dread that hit me now was deeper, more fundamental, more structurally devastating. It was the dread of understanding that the one being in existence who possessed the power to challenge Godric was Godric himself, from the future, and that this future Godric was not a rival, not an opponent, not a counterbalance, but a guarantee. A guarantee that Godric's power would never plateau, never diminish, never be countered, never be exploited, never be surpassed by any external force. The one being who exceeded current Godric was future Godric, and future Godric's existence was the mechanism that made current Godric's power irrevocable, and future Godric's power was, in turn, made irrevocable by an even more distant future Godric, the chain extending forever, each link forging the previous one into absolute permanence.

There was no exploiting it. There was no attacking through it. There was no reproducing it, no understanding it in time, no using it as a path to victory. The limitation was sealed by a being so powerful that the shadow of his afterthought could negate the combined power of every Godric across all timelines, and that being was Godric, later, more powerful, more absolute. The one weakness was inward, temporal, and structurally self-reinforcing. It was a door that could only be opened by a key that was inside the door, and the door was inside the key, and both were made of the same material, and the material was Godric.

I thought about the implications, and each thought was a blade. The amplification could never be stopped, because its creator was unstoppable. The Weakness Paradox could never be turned against Godric, because its author was beyond the reach of any paradox. The absolute concealment could never be pierced, because the being who designed it existed beyond the capacity of any force to perceive. The immune system could never be bypassed, because the being who engineered it was beyond the capacity of any force to harm. Every mechanism, every system, every power, was anchored in the will of a future Godric so incomprehensibly more powerful than the current omniverse that the shadow of his afterthought could negate the combined power of every iteration of Godric across all timelines, and the only way to challenge that will was to be an even more distant future Godric, whose will was, in turn, anchored by an even more distant future Godric, the chain extending forever, each link more unassailable than the last.

The unfairness of it was so absolute, so total, so structurally complete, that it transcended the concept of unfairness and became something else, something that had no name because no language had ever needed to describe a state of existence so grotesquely, so obscenely, so cosmically one-sided. Every being in the omniverse, every entity that had ever existed or would ever exist, was trapped inside a system designed and maintained by a being who was, by definition, the most powerful thing that would ever exist, and whose power was guaranteed to grow forever, and whose guarantee was itself guaranteed by an even more powerful version of the same being, the chain of guarantees extending infinitely, each one more unassailable than the last, the entire structure closed, sealed, permanent, and absolutely, utterly, forever unchallengeable.

I tried to imagine the scale of future Godric. I had tried before, in the garden, when he had asked me to guess what releasing more than zero percent of his power would look like, and my guess had been dismissed as not even close to the effectively-zero-percent output of his weakest newborn aura. That was the current Godric. The being sitting in front of me, the most powerful entity in the omniverse by an infinite margin, was to future Godric what a single dead skin cell was to the combined mass of every universe that had ever existed. And future Godric's shadow of an afterthought had negated the combined power of every Godric across all timelines. The scale was not merely incomprehensible. It was hostile to comprehension. It was a number so vast that the mathematics of vastness broke trying to contain it, and the being who possessed that number was the same being who had designed the amplification that made current Godric omnipotent, and the design was so far beyond current Godric's capacity to understand that the understanding itself was a structural impossibility.

Why had future Godric created the Stasis Event? The question surfaced unbidden, rising from the wreckage of my shattered frameworks like a bubble from a drowned man's lips. I did not ask it aloud. I did not need to. Godric's omniscience had already perceived it, and he answered it with the careful, measured tone of a man who had contemplated the question for a million years and arrived at conclusions he acknowledged as speculation.

"I do not know his motive," Godric said. "I know what he did. I know how he did it. I know the mechanics of the Stasis Event, its structure, its implementation, its irrevocability. But the why, the reason a being so incomprehensibly powerful that his afterthought can negate the combined existence of every version of himself across all timelines, would reach backward across an unimaginable span of time to transform a mortal king's grief into the genesis of an apex predator, that I cannot say with certainty. I have speculated. Perhaps he wanted to ensure his own existence, to create the chain of causation that would lead to his birth. Perhaps he wanted to give his past self the power to protect what he could not protect as a mortal. Perhaps, for a being so vast that the omniverse is a soap bubble, the act of reaching back and reshaping a single moment of his own history was simply interesting, a creative act, the way a sculptor might shape clay, not out of necessity but out of the desire to create something. Perhaps all of these. Perhaps none. I do not know, and the not knowing does not trouble me, because I am him, and he is me, and whatever reason he had, it was sufficient for a being whose sufficiency is beyond question."

He smiled, and it was the warm, contented smile of a man who had made peace with the greatest mystery of his existence.

"I should clarify," he added, his voice carrying a note of gentle, almost protective emphasis, "that future Godric did not cause the fall of Olympus. He did not orchestrate the attack. He did not manipulate Thronegate into betraying us. He did not arrange the deaths of Reina, Isabel, Hebe, or Thorin. Those tragedies were genuine. They were real. They were caused by the ambition and cruelty of mortal Thronegate, by the political vulnerability of a small, noble kingdom that could not withstand a surprise attack from a larger neighbor. Future Godric used Thorin's spell as the delivery point for the Stasis Event, but he did not cause the circumstances that led to the spell being cast. He took a genuine tragedy and transformed its aftermath into the genesis of my power. He did not create the grief. He forged the grief into a weapon."

Godric's eyes softened, and for a moment, a single, fractional moment, I saw the mortal king beneath the god, the man who had stood on a marble dais in jasmine-scented air and watched his daughter approach an altar, the man who had loved deeply and lost everything. The moment passed, and the warm, comfortable, amused expression returned, the expression of a being who had processed the worst thing that had ever happened to him and emerged from the processing not merely intact but satisfied.

"I am not lonely, Kaedan," he said. "I am not burdened. I am not conflicted. I am a man who lost everything he loved, and who was given, by his own future hand, the power to ensure that nothing he chooses to protect will ever be threatened again. The grief remains. The loss remains. But the power that grew from it is absolute, and its absoluteness is guaranteed by the most reliable source in existence: myself, from a future so distant it cannot be written. I have accepted the limitation because the limitation is not a limitation. It is a promise. A promise that I will never stop growing, that my power will never be surpassed, that every mechanism of my supremacy is anchored in a will so vast that no force in existence can challenge it. The one thing I cannot do is the one thing that proves I will always be able to do everything else."

He stood. The movement was fluid, effortless, the movement of a body that had been perfectly maintained for a million years without a single moment of deterioration. He walked to the shelf, took down the cedar box of pipe tobacco, held it beneath his nose again, and inhaled. He closed the box. He turned back to me, and his face was the face of the man I had served for forty-seven years, the face of Uncle Godric, the warm, kind, approachable, protective, beloved uncle who dimmed lanterns and inquired about knee pains and ruffled the hair of children.

"You have served me faithfully for half a century, Kaedan," he said. "You have read the journals. You have witnessed my power. And now you know the one thing I have told no one else. The one limitation. The one truth that completes the picture. I trust you with it because I trust you, and because your father trusted you, and because Blake, who found me in the dark ten thousand years before you were born, would want his descendant to understand the full scope of what he served."

He walked over to me and placed his warm, calloused hand on my shoulder. The same hand that could crush the omniverse. The same hand that had been guided to my shoulder by a being whose omniscience was absolute, whose power was guaranteed by a future version of himself so vast that the shadow of his afterthought could negate the combined existence of every Godric across all timelines.

"You look terrified," Godric said, and his voice was gentle, concerned, the voice of a favorite uncle noticing that his nephew had gone pale. "I am sorry. I did not mean to upset you. I forget, sometimes, that these truths are not comfortable for mortal minds. I have lived with them for so long that they feel like furniture. But you are not me, and I should have been more gentle."

He squeezed my shoulder. The grip was warm. Solid. Grounding. The grip of a man who loved his court wizard, who valued his companionship, who kept him close not because he needed protection but because his presence gave meaning to a million years of infinite, recursive, accelerating supremacy.

"Come," he said. "Let us get you cleaned up. You are bleeding on my study rug, and it is a very old rug. Blake gave it to me."

I stood. My legs carried me. My body moved. I followed him out of the study and into the corridor, where the evening light was warm and golden and exactly as it had always been, because Godric had decided it should be. I followed him because there was nowhere else to go. There was nowhere else that existed. There was only Godric, and the corridor, and the golden light, and the absolute, permanent, irrevocable certainty that the being walking beside me was the most powerful thing in existence, and that his power was guaranteed by a future version of himself that was so incomprehensibly more powerful that the shadow of his afterthought could negate every Godric across all timelines, and that this chain of guarantees extended forever, each link more unassailable than the last, and that no force in any reality, any timeline, any dimension, any conceptual framework of existence, would ever, could ever, find a crack, a gap, a fissure, a weakness, a limitation, a single, infinitesimal, microscopic point of vulnerability through which opposition could be even theoretically contemplated.

The one weakness was himself. The one limitation was his own future supremacy. The one thing he could not do was the one thing that proved he could do everything else, forever, without end, without challenge, without the possibility, the theoretical framework, the conceptual architecture within which the possibility could be formulated, of ever being opposed.

I walked beside him. The blood dried on my lip. The evening light was warm. And the existential dread, the absolute, final, total, structural, irrevocable, permanent dread of understanding that the apex predator of all creation had no weakness, not even the one he claimed to have, because the weakness was himself, and himself was the strongest thing in existence, and the strongest thing in existence was guaranteed to grow stronger forever, settled into my bones like lead, like ice, like the cold, dense, absolute weight of a truth that would never leave me, that would never diminish, that would sit inside my marrow for the remaining years of my mortal life and whisper, in the quiet hours of the night, that the being I served, the being I loved, the being who dimmed my lanterns and asked about my mother's knee, was not merely unbeatable.

He was structurally, permanently, recursively, infinitely, and forever unbeatable, and the guarantee of his unbeatability was himself, and himself was forever, and forever was him, and there was nothing, anywhere, in any framework of existence, that would ever be anything other than what he permitted it to be.

I followed him down the corridor. I did not speak. I would not speak for three days. When I finally did, it would be to ask the palace cook to prepare extra rosemary in the evening lamb, because Uncle Godric preferred it that way, and what Uncle Godric preferred was what the omniverse would provide, and what the omniverse would provide was what Uncle Godric permitted, and what Uncle Godric permitted was everything, because there was nothing he did not permit, except the one thing he could not permit, and the one thing he could not permit was the alteration of his own past, and the reason he could not permit it was that his future self, a being so vast that afterthoughts could negate the combined power of every iteration of himself across all timelines, had decided, for reasons known only to a being beyond comprehension, that the grief of a mortal king in a forgotten kingdom called Olympus would be the seed from which an infinite, recursive, eternally ascending god would grow.

And the seed had been planted. And the god had grown. And the god was growing still. And the god would grow forever. And nothing, anywhere, in any reality, any timeline, any dimension, any concept, any dream, any nightmare, any prayer, any curse, any desperate, clawing, agonizing hope that the universe might contain something, anything, that could stand against him, would ever be anything other than what it was.

Nothing.
